<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Continent</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Very large landmass identified by convention</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Continent_(disambiguation)" title="Continent (disambiguation)">Continent (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Continental_models-Australia.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="755" data-file-width="1488" decoding="async" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Continental_models-Australia.gif/350px-Continental_models-Australia.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Continental_models-Australia.gif/525px-Continental_models-Australia.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Continental_models-Australia.gif/700px-Continental_models-Australia.gif 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Continental_models-Australia.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Animated, colour-coded map showing the various continents. Depending on <a href="/wiki/Boundaries_between_the_continents_of_Earth" title="Boundaries between the continents of Earth">the convention and model</a>, some continents may be consolidated or subdivided.</div></div></div>
<p>A <b>continent</b> is any of several large <a href="/wiki/Landmass" title="Landmass">landmasses</a>. Generally identified by <a href="/wiki/Convention_(norm)" title="Convention (norm)">convention</a> rather than any strict criteria, up to seven geographical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Regions" title="Regions">regions</a> are commonly regarded as continents. Ordered from largest in area to smallest, these seven regions are:  <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Australia_(continent)" title="Australia (continent)">Australia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NatlGeo1_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-NatlGeo1-1">[1]</a></sup> Variations with fewer continents may merge some of these, for example <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">America</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Afro-Eurasia" title="Afro-Eurasia">Afro-Eurasia</a> are sometimes treated as single continents, which can bring the total number as low as four. <a href="/wiki/Zealandia" title="Zealandia">Zealandia</a>, a largely submerged mass of continental crust, has also been described as a continent.
</p><p>Oceanic islands are frequently grouped with a nearby continent to divide all the world's land into geographical regions. Under this scheme, most of the island countries and territories in the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a> are grouped together with the continent of Australia to form a geographical region called <i><a href="/wiki/Oceania" title="Oceania">Oceania</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">geology</a>, a continent is defined as "one of <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>'s major landmasses, including both dry <a href="/wiki/Land" title="Land">land</a> and <a href="/wiki/Continental_shelf" title="Continental shelf">continental shelves</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glossary2005_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Glossary2005-3">[3]</a></sup> The geological continents correspond to six large areas of <a href="/wiki/Continental_crust" title="Continental crust">continental crust</a> that are found on the <a href="/wiki/List_of_tectonic_plates" title="List of tectonic plates">tectonic plates</a>, but exclude small <a href="/wiki/Continental_fragment" title="Continental fragment">continental fragments</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a> that are generally referred to as microcontinents. Continental crust is only known to exist on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>The idea of <a href="/wiki/Continental_drift" title="Continental drift">continental drift</a> gained recognition in the 20th century. It postulates that the current continents formed from the breaking up of a supercontinent <a href="/wiki/Pangaea" title="Pangaea">Pangaea</a> that formed hundreds of millions of years ago.
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions_and_application">Definitions and application <span class="anchor" id="Definition"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Continent&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Definitions and application">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Island#Differentiation_from_continents" title="Island">Island § Differentiation from continents</a></div>
<p>By convention, continents "are understood to be large, continuous, discrete masses of land, ideally separated by expanses of water".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewis_&amp;_Wigen,_The_Myth_of_Continents199721_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewis_&amp;_Wigen,_The_Myth_of_Continents199721-5">[5]</a></sup> In modern schemes with five or more recognised continents, at least one pair of continents is joined by land in some way. The criterion "large" leads to arbitrary classification: <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a>, with a surface area of 2,166,086 square kilometres (836,330 sq mi), is only considered the world's largest island, while <a href="/wiki/Australia_(continent)" title="Australia (continent)">Australia</a>, at 7,617,930 square kilometres (2,941,300 sq mi), is deemed the smallest continent.
</p><p>Earth's major landmasses all have coasts on a single, continuous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Ocean" title="World Ocean">World Ocean</a>, which is divided into a number of principal <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">oceanic</a> components by the continents and various geographic criteria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNAoO_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNAoO-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Extent">Extent</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Continent&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Extent">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The most restricted meaning of <i>continent</i> is that of a continuous<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Primary,_secondary_and_tertiary_sources" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable secondary sources. (October 2021)">non-tertiary source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> area of land or mainland, with the coastline and any land boundaries forming the edge of the continent. In this sense, the term <i><a href="/wiki/Continental_Europe" title="Continental Europe">continental Europe</a></i> (sometimes referred to in Britain as "the Continent") is used to refer to mainland Europe, excluding <a href="/wiki/Island" title="Island">islands</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Malta" title="Malta">Malta</a> while the term <i>continent of Australia</i> may refer to the <a href="/wiki/Mainland_Australia" title="Mainland Australia">mainland of Australia</a>, excluding <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">New Guinea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tasmania" title="Tasmania">Tasmania</a>, and other nearby islands. Similarly, the <i><a href="/wiki/Contiguous_United_States" title="Contiguous United States">continental United States</a></i> refers to the 48 contiguous states and the <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">District of Columbia</a> and may include <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a> in the northwest of the continent (the two being separated by <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>), while excluding <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a>.
</p><p>From the perspective of <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">geology</a> or <a href="/wiki/Physical_geography" title="Physical geography">physical geography</a>, <i>continent</i> may be extended beyond the confines of continuous dry land to include the shallow, submerged adjacent area (the <a href="/wiki/Continental_shelf" title="Continental shelf">continental shelf</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> and the islands on the shelf (<a href="/wiki/Island#Continental_islands" title="Island">continental islands</a>), as they are structurally part of the continent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>From this perspective, the edge of the continental shelf is the true edge of the continent, as shorelines vary with changes in sea level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ollier_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ollier-11">[11]</a></sup> In this sense the islands of Great Britain and Ireland are part of Europe, while Australia and the island of New Guinea together form a continent.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Island_nations.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="443" data-file-width="863" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Island_nations.svg/330px-Island_nations.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Island_nations.svg/495px-Island_nations.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Island_nations.svg/660px-Island_nations.svg.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Island_nations.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Island_countries" title="Island countries">island countries</a>: these states are often grouped geographically with a neighbouring continental landmass</div></div></div>
<p>As a <a href="/wiki/Social_constructionism" title="Social constructionism">cultural construct</a>, the concept of a continent may go beyond the continental shelf to include <a href="/wiki/Island#Oceanic_islands" title="Island">oceanic islands</a> and continental fragments. In this way, Iceland is considered part of Europe, and <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a> part of Africa. Extrapolating the concept to its extreme, some geographers group the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Plate" title="Australian Plate">Australian continental plate</a> with other islands in the Pacific into one "quasi-continent" called <a href="/wiki/Oceania" title="Oceania">Oceania</a>. This divides the entire land surface of Earth into continents or quasi-continents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Separation">Separation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Continent&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Separation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Boundaries_between_the_continents_of_Earth" title="Boundaries between the continents of Earth">Boundaries between the continents of Earth</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_transcontinental_countries" title="List of transcontinental countries">List of transcontinental countries</a></div>
<p>The criterion that each continent is a discrete landmass is commonly relaxed due to historical conventions and practical use. Of the seven most globally recognized continents, only Antarctica and Australia are completely separated from other continents by the ocean. Several continents are defined not as absolutely distinct bodies but as "<i>more or less</i> discrete masses of land".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewis_&amp;_Wigen,_The_Myth_of_Continents199735_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewis_&amp;_Wigen,_The_Myth_of_Continents199735-13">[13]</a></sup> Asia and Africa are joined by the <a href="/wiki/Isthmus_of_Suez" title="Isthmus of Suez">Isthmus of Suez</a>, and North and South America by the <a href="/wiki/Isthmus_of_Panama" title="Isthmus of Panama">Isthmus of Panama</a>. In both cases, there is no complete separation of these landmasses by water (disregarding the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal" title="Suez Canal">Suez Canal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Panama_Canal" title="Panama Canal">Panama Canal</a>, which are both narrow and shallow, as well as man-made). Both of these <a href="/wiki/Isthmus" title="Isthmus">isthmuses</a> are very narrow compared to the bulk of the landmasses they unite.
</p><p>North America and South America are treated as separate continents in the seven-continent model. However, they may also be viewed as a single continent known as <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">America</a>. This viewpoint was common in the United States until <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, and remains prevalent in some Asian six-continent models.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> The single American continent model remains the more common view in France, Germany, Greece, Hungary, Italy, Malta, Portugal, Spain, and Latin American countries.
</p><p>The criterion of a discrete landmass is completely disregarded if the continuous landmass of Eurasia is classified as two separate continents (Europe and Asia). Physiographically, Europe and the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a> are peninsulas of the Eurasian landmass. However, Europe is widely considered a continent with its comparatively large land area of 10,180,000 square kilometres (3,930,000 sq mi), while the Indian subcontinent, with less than half that area, is considered a subcontinent. The alternative view—in geology and geography—that Eurasia is a single continent results in a six-continent view of the world. Some view separation of Eurasia into Asia and Europe as a residue of <a href="/wiki/Eurocentrism" title="Eurocentrism">Eurocentrism</a>: "In physical, cultural and historical diversity, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> and <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> are comparable to the entire European landmass, not to a single European country. [...]."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewis_&amp;_Wigen,_The_Myth_of_Continents1997104–123_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewis_&amp;_Wigen,_The_Myth_of_Continents1997104–123-15">[15]</a></sup> However, for historical and cultural reasons, the view of Europe as a separate continent continues in several categorizations.
</p><p>If continents are defined strictly as discrete landmasses, embracing all the contiguous land of a body, then Africa, Asia, and Europe form a single continent which may be referred to as <a href="/wiki/Afro-Eurasia" title="Afro-Eurasia">Afro-Eurasia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McColl_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-McColl-16">[16]</a></sup> Combined with the consolidation of the Americas, this would produce a four-continent model consisting of Afro-Eurasia, America, Antarctica and Australia.
</p><p>When sea levels were lower during the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a> <a href="/wiki/Ice_age" title="Ice age">ice ages</a>, greater areas of continental shelf were exposed as dry land, forming <a href="/wiki/Land_bridge" title="Land bridge">land bridges</a> between <a href="/wiki/Tasmania" title="Tasmania">Tasmania</a> and the Australian mainland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> At those times Australia and New Guinea were a single, continuous continent known as <a href="/wiki/Sahul" title="Sahul">Sahul</a>. Likewise, the Americas and Afro-Eurasia were joined by the <a href="/wiki/Beringia" title="Beringia">Bering Land Bridge</a>. Other islands, such as Great Britain, were joined to the mainlands of their continents. At that time, there were just three discrete landmasses: Afro-Eurasia-America, Antarctica, and Sahul.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Number">Number</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Continent&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Number">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><span class="anchor" id="Number"></span>
There are several ways of distinguishing the continents:

</td>
<td><span style="font-size:85%;"><span style="background:#0040ff; border:0px solid #000; vertical-align:center; padding-right:1em; padding-top:0ex; padding-bottom:0ex; line-height:100%"> </span></span> Antarctica
</td>
<td><span style="font-size:85%;"><span style="background:#c04080; border:0px solid #000; vertical-align:center; padding-right:1em; padding-top:0ex; padding-bottom:0ex; line-height:100%"> </span></span> Australia
</td>
<td><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-EB-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NatlGeo2_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-NatlGeo2-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AoCA_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-AoCA-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford1_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford1-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encarta_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Encarta-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford2_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford2-38">[38]</a></sup>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<ul><li>The seven-continent model is usually taught in most <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_English-speaking_population" title="List of countries by English-speaking population">English-speaking countries</a> including <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> and also in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>, and parts of <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a>.</li>
<li>The six-continent combined-Eurasia model is mostly used in <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>.</li>
<li>The six-continent combined-America model is taught in countries that speak <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">Romance languages</a>, it is also often used in <a href="/wiki/Suriname" title="Suriname">Suriname</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guyana" title="Guyana">Guyana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belize" title="Belize">Belize</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HEL_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-HEL-33">[33]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Olympic_symbols#Different_types_of_flags" title="Olympic symbols">Olympic flag</a>'s<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OC_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-OC-41">[41]</a></sup> five rings represent the five inhabited continents of the combined-America model, which excludes Antarctica.</li></ul>
<p>As previously mentioned, some geographers use the name <a href="/wiki/Oceania" title="Oceania">Oceania</a> for a region including most of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Island_countries" title="Island countries">island countries</a> and territories in the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a> as well as the <a href="/wiki/Australia_(continent)" title="Australia (continent)">continent of Australia</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Area_and_population">Area and population</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Continent&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Area and population">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><span class="anchor" id="List"></span>
<span class="anchor" id="Area_and_population_table"></span>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For a more detailed list of populations by continental regions and subregions, see <a href="/wiki/List_of_continents_and_continental_subregions_by_population" title="List of continents and continental subregions by population">List of continents and continental subregions by population</a>.</div>
<p>The following table provides areas given by <i>Encyclopædia Britannica</i> for each continent in accordance with the seven-continent model, including Australia along with <a href="/wiki/Melanesia" title="Melanesia">Melanesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Micronesia" title="Micronesia">Micronesia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Polynesia" title="Polynesia">Polynesia</a> as part of Oceania. It also provides populations of continents according to 2021 estimates by the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Statistics_Division" title="United Nations Statistics Division">United Nations Statistics Division</a> based on the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_geoscheme" title="United Nations geoscheme">UN geoscheme</a>, which includes all of Russia (i.e., including <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>) as part of Europe, but all of Azerbaijan, Cyprus, Georgia, Kazakhstan, and Turkey (i.e., including <a href="/wiki/East_Thrace" title="East Thrace">East Thrace</a>) as part of Asia.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_divisions">Other divisions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Continent&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Other divisions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Supercontinents">Supercontinents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Continent&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Supercontinents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Supercontinent" title="Supercontinent">Supercontinent</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Geological_history_of_Earth" title="Geological history of Earth">Geological history of Earth</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:322px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pangaea_200Ma.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="759" data-file-width="1519" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Pangaea_200Ma.jpg/320px-Pangaea_200Ma.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Pangaea_200Ma.jpg/480px-Pangaea_200Ma.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Pangaea_200Ma.jpg/640px-Pangaea_200Ma.jpg 2x" width="320"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pangaea_200Ma.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Reconstruction of the supercontinent <a href="/wiki/Pangaea" title="Pangaea">Pangaea</a> approximately 200 million years ago.</div></div></div>
<p>Apart from the current continents, the scope and meaning of the term <i>continent</i> includes past geological ones. <a href="/wiki/Supercontinent" title="Supercontinent">Supercontinents</a>, largely in evidence earlier in the geological record, are landmasses that comprise most of the world's <a href="/wiki/Craton" title="Craton">cratons</a> or continental cores.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Evans_2013_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Evans_2013-52">[52]</a></sup> These have included <a href="/wiki/Vaalbara" title="Vaalbara">Vaalbara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kenorland" title="Kenorland">Kenorland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Columbia_(supercontinent)" title="Columbia (supercontinent)">Columbia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rodinia" title="Rodinia">Rodinia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pannotia" title="Pannotia">Pannotia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pangaea" title="Pangaea">Pangaea</a>. Over time, these supercontinents broke apart into large landmasses which formed the present continents.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Subcontinents">Subcontinents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Continent&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Subcontinents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:India_78.40398E_20.74980N.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="958" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/India_78.40398E_20.74980N.jpg/220px-India_78.40398E_20.74980N.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/India_78.40398E_20.74980N.jpg/330px-India_78.40398E_20.74980N.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/India_78.40398E_20.74980N.jpg/440px-India_78.40398E_20.74980N.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:India_78.40398E_20.74980N.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a></div></div></div>
<p>Certain parts of continents are recognized as subcontinents, especially the large peninsulas separated from the main continental landmass by geographical features. The most widely recognized example is the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baldwin_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baldwin-53">[53]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Cone" title="Southern Cone">Southern Cone</a> of South America, and <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a> in North America might be considered further examples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baldwin_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-Baldwin-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>In many of these cases, the "subcontinents" concerned are on different <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">tectonic plates</a> from the rest of the continent, providing a geological justification for the terminology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Molnar2015_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Molnar2015-54">[54]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a>, generally reckoned as the <a href="/wiki/List_of_islands_by_area" title="List of islands by area">world's largest island</a> on the northeastern periphery of the <a href="/wiki/North_American_Plate" title="North American Plate">North American Plate</a>, is sometimes referred to as a subcontinent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FarmerCook2013_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FarmerCook2013-56">[56]</a></sup> This is a significant departure from the more conventional view of a subcontinent as comprising a very large peninsula on the fringe of a continent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baldwin_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-Baldwin-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>Where the Americas are viewed as a single continent (America), it is divided into two subcontinents (<a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gallay2015_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gallay2015-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-InnesHaron2000_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-InnesHaron2000-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vivares2014_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vivares2014-59">[59]</a></sup> or three (<a href="/wiki/Central_America" title="Central America">Central America</a> being the third).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leonard2005_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Leonard2005-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> When Eurasia is regarded as a single continent, Europe is treated as a subcontinent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baldwin_53-3"><a href="#cite_note-Baldwin-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Submerged_continents">Submerged continents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Continent&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Submerged continents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Submerged_continent" title="Submerged continent">Submerged continent</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_lost_lands" title="List of lost lands">List of lost lands</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Zealandia" title="Zealandia">Zealandia</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zealandia,_topographic_map.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1700" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="249" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Zealandia%2C_topographic_map.jpg/220px-Zealandia%2C_topographic_map.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Zealandia%2C_topographic_map.jpg/330px-Zealandia%2C_topographic_map.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Zealandia%2C_topographic_map.jpg/440px-Zealandia%2C_topographic_map.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zealandia,_topographic_map.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Zealandia" title="Zealandia">Zealandia</a>, the largest submerged landmass or continent</div></div></div>
<p>Some areas of <a href="/wiki/Continental_crust" title="Continental crust">continental crust</a> are largely covered by the ocean and may be considered <a href="/wiki/Submerged_continent" title="Submerged continent">submerged continents</a>. Notable examples are <a href="/wiki/Zealandia" title="Zealandia">Zealandia</a>, emerging from the ocean primarily in <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Caledonia" title="New Caledonia">New Caledonia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Primary,_secondary_and_tertiary_sources" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable secondary sources. (October 2021)">non-tertiary source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and the almost completely submerged <a href="/wiki/Kerguelen_Plateau" title="Kerguelen Plateau">Kerguelen Plateau</a> in the southern <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Microcontinents">Microcontinents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Continent&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Microcontinents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Continental_fragment" title="Continental fragment">Continental fragment</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a></div>
<p>Some islands lie on sections of continental crust that have rifted and drifted apart from a main continental landmass. While not considered continents because of their relatively small size, they may be considered <a href="/wiki/Continental_fragment" title="Continental fragment">microcontinents</a>. <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a>, the largest example, is usually considered an island of Africa, but its divergent evolution has caused it to be referred to as "the eighth continent" from a biological perspective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_the_concept">History of the concept</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Continent&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: History of the concept">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_concepts_of_the_Old_World_continents">Early concepts of the Old World continents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Continent&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Early concepts of the Old World continents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Strabo.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2001" data-file-width="1657" decoding="async" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Strabo.jpg/170px-Strabo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Strabo.jpg/255px-Strabo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Strabo.jpg/340px-Strabo.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Strabo.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Ancient Greek geographer <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a> holding a globe showing <i>Europa</i> and <i>Asia</i></div></div></div><p>The term "continent" translates the Greek word <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">ἤπειρος</span></span>, meaning "landmass, terra firma", the proper name of <a href="/wiki/Epirus" title="Epirus">Epirus</a> and later especially used for Asia (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Asia Minor</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first distinction between continents was made by <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greek</a> mariners who gave the names Europe and Asia to the lands on either side of the waterways of the <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Sea" title="Aegean Sea">Aegean Sea</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Dardanelles" title="Dardanelles">Dardanelles</a> strait, the <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Marmara" title="Sea of Marmara">Sea of Marmara</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Bosporus" title="Bosporus">Bosporus</a> strait and the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Toynbee_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Toynbee-66">[66]</a></sup> The names were first applied just to lands near the coast and only later extended to include the <a href="/wiki/Hinterland" title="Hinterland">hinterlands</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pocock2002_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pocock2002-68">[68]</a></sup> But the division was only carried through to the end of navigable waterways and "... beyond that point the Hellenic geographers never succeeded in laying their finger on any inland feature in the physical landscape that could offer any convincing line for partitioning an indivisible Eurasia ..."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Toynbee_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-Toynbee-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ancient Greek thinkers subsequently debated whether Africa (then called <i>Libya</i>) should be considered part of Asia or a third part of the world. Division into three parts eventually came to predominate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> From the Greek viewpoint, the Aegean Sea was the center of the world; Asia lay to the east, Europe to the north and west, and Africa to the south.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewis_&amp;_Wigen,_The_Myth_of_Continents199721–22_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewis_&amp;_Wigen,_The_Myth_of_Continents199721–22-70">[70]</a></sup> The boundaries between the continents were not fixed. Early on, the Europe–Asia boundary was taken to run from the Black Sea along the <a href="/wiki/Rioni" title="Rioni">Rioni</a> River (known then as the <i>Phasis</i>) in <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a>. Later it was viewed as running from the Black Sea through <a href="/wiki/Kerch_Strait" title="Kerch Strait">Kerch Strait</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Azov" title="Sea of Azov">Sea of Azov</a> and along the <a href="/wiki/Don_(river)" title="Don (river)">Don</a> River (known then as the <i>Tanais</i>) in <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> The boundary between Asia and Africa was generally taken to be the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a> River. <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> in the 5th century BC objected to the whole of <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> being split between Asia and Africa ("Libya") and took the boundary to lie along the western border of Egypt, regarding Egypt as part of Asia. He also questioned the division into three of what is really a single landmass,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> a debate that continues nearly two and a half millennia later.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Eratosthenes" title="Eratosthenes">Eratosthenes</a>, in the 3rd century BC, noted that some geographers divided the continents by rivers (the Nile and the Don), thus considering them "islands". Others divided the continents by <a href="/wiki/Isthmus" title="Isthmus">isthmuses</a>, calling the continents "peninsulas". These latter geographers set the border between Europe and Asia at the isthmus between the Black Sea and the <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a>, and the border between Asia and Africa at the isthmus between the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> and the mouth of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Bardawil" title="Lake Bardawil">Lake Bardawil</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:T_and_O_map_Guntherus_Ziner_1472.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="864" data-file-width="864" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/T_and_O_map_Guntherus_Ziner_1472.jpg/220px-T_and_O_map_Guntherus_Ziner_1472.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/T_and_O_map_Guntherus_Ziner_1472.jpg/330px-T_and_O_map_Guntherus_Ziner_1472.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/T_and_O_map_Guntherus_Ziner_1472.jpg/440px-T_and_O_map_Guntherus_Ziner_1472.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:T_and_O_map_Guntherus_Ziner_1472.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Medieval <a href="/wiki/T_and_O_map" title="T and O map">T and O map</a> showing the three continents as domains of the sons of <a href="/wiki/Noah" title="Noah">Noah</a>—Asia to Sem (<a href="/wiki/Shem" title="Shem">Shem</a>), Europe to Iafeth (<a href="/wiki/Japheth" title="Japheth">Japheth</a>), and Africa to Cham (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ham,_son_of_Noah" title="Ham, son of Noah">Ham</a>).</div></div></div>
<p>The Roman Empire did not attach a strong identity to these continental divisions. However, following the fall of the <a href="/wiki/Western_Roman_Empire" title="Western Roman Empire">Western Roman Empire</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">culture that developed in its place</a>, linked to Latin and the Catholic church, began to associate itself with the concept of "Europe".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pocock2002_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pocock2002-68">[68]</a></sup> Through the Roman period and the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, a few writers took the <a href="/wiki/Isthmus_of_Suez" title="Isthmus of Suez">Isthmus of Suez</a> as the boundary between Asia and Africa, but most writers continued to consider it the Nile or the western border of Egypt (Gibbon).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In the Middle Ages, the world was usually portrayed on <a href="/wiki/T_and_O_map" title="T and O map">T and O maps</a>, with the T representing the waters dividing the three continents. By the middle of the 18th century, "the fashion of dividing Asia and Africa at the Nile, or at the Great Catabathmus [the boundary between Egypt and <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a>] farther west, had even then scarcely passed away".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="European_arrival_in_the_Americas">European arrival in the Americas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Continent&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: European arrival in the Americas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">Christopher Columbus</a> sailed across the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">West Indies</a> in 1492, sparking a period of European exploration of the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a>. But despite four voyages to the Americas, Columbus never believed he had reached a new continent—he always thought it was part of Asia.
</p><p>In 1501, <a href="/wiki/Amerigo_Vespucci" title="Amerigo Vespucci">Amerigo Vespucci</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gon%C3%A7alo_Coelho" title="Gonçalo Coelho">Gonçalo Coelho</a> attempted to sail around what they considered the southern end of the Asian mainland into the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a>, passing through <a href="/wiki/Fernando_de_Noronha" title="Fernando de Noronha">Fernando de Noronha</a>. After reaching the coast of <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, they sailed a long way farther south along the coast of <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a>, confirming that this was a land of continental proportions and that it also extended much farther south than Asia was known to.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OGorman_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-OGorman-76">[76]</a></sup> On return to Europe, an account of the voyage, called <i>Mundus Novus</i> ("New World"), was published under Vespucci's name in 1502 or 1503,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-formisano1_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-formisano1-77">[77]</a></sup> although it seems that it had additions or alterations by another writer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zerubavel1_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zerubavel1-78">[78]</a></sup> Regardless of who penned the words, <i>Mundus Novus</i> credited Vespucci with saying, "I have discovered a continent in those southern regions that is inhabited by more numerous people and animals than our Europe, or Asia or Africa",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-formisano2_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-formisano2-79">[79]</a></sup> the first known explicit identification of part of the Americas as a continent like the other three.
</p><p>Within a few years, the name "New World" began appearing as a name for South America on world maps, such as the Oliveriana (Pesaro) map of around 1504–1505. Maps of this time, though, still showed <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a> connected to Asia and showed South America as a separate land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zerubavel1_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zerubavel1-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Waldseemuller_map_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="7590" data-file-width="13708" decoding="async" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Waldseemuller_map_2.jpg/310px-Waldseemuller_map_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Waldseemuller_map_2.jpg/465px-Waldseemuller_map_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Waldseemuller_map_2.jpg/620px-Waldseemuller_map_2.jpg 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Waldseemuller_map_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Universalis Cosmographia</i>, Waldseemüller's 1507 world map—the first to show the Americas separate from Asia</div></div></div>
<p>In 1507 <a href="/wiki/Martin_Waldseem%C3%BCller" title="Martin Waldseemüller">Martin Waldseemüller</a> published a world map, <i><a href="/wiki/Waldseem%C3%BCller_map" title="Waldseemüller map">Universalis Cosmographia</a></i>, which was the first to show North and South America as separate from Asia and surrounded by water. A small inset map above the main map explicitly showed for the first time the Americas being east of Asia and separated from Asia by an ocean, as opposed to just placing the Americas on the left end of the map and Asia on the right end. In the accompanying book <i><a href="/wiki/Cosmographiae_Introductio" title="Cosmographiae Introductio">Cosmographiae Introductio</a></i>, Waldseemüller noted that the earth is divided into four parts, Europe, Asia, Africa, and the fourth part, which he named "America" after Amerigo Vespucci's first name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zerubavel2_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zerubavel2-80">[80]</a></sup> On the map, the word "America" was placed on part of South America.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_word_continent">The word <i>continent</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Continent&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: The word continent">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>From the 16th century the English noun <i>continent</i> was derived from the term <i>continent land</i>, meaning continuous or connected land<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oed_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-oed-81">[81]</a></sup> and translated from the Latin <i>terra continens</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> The noun was used to mean "a connected or continuous tract of land" or <a href="/wiki/Mainland" title="Mainland">mainland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oed_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-oed-81">[81]</a></sup> It was not applied only to very large areas of land—in the 17th century, references were made to the <i>continents</i> (or mainlands) of <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">Isle of Man</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> and in 1745 to <a href="/wiki/Sumatra" title="Sumatra">Sumatra</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oed_81-2"><a href="#cite_note-oed-81">[81]</a></sup> The word <i>continent</i> was used in translating Greek and Latin writings about the three "parts" of the world, although in the original languages no word of exactly the same meaning as <i>continent</i> was used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewis_&amp;_Wigen,_The_Myth_of_Continents199729_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewis_&amp;_Wigen,_The_Myth_of_Continents199729-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>While <i>continent</i> was used on the one hand for relatively small areas of continuous land, on the other hand geographers again raised Herodotus's query about why a single large landmass should be divided into separate continents. In the mid-17th century, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peter_Heylin" title="Peter Heylin">Peter Heylin</a> wrote in his <i>Cosmographie</i> that "A Continent is a great quantity of Land, not separated by any Sea from the rest of the World, as the whole Continent of Europe, Asia, Africa." In 1727, <a href="/wiki/Ephraim_Chambers" title="Ephraim Chambers">Ephraim Chambers</a> wrote in his <i>Cyclopædia,</i> "The world is ordinarily divided into two grand continents: the <a href="/wiki/Old_World" title="Old World">old</a> and the <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">new</a>." And in his 1752 atlas, <a href="/wiki/Emanuel_Bowen" title="Emanuel Bowen">Emanuel Bowen</a> defined a continent as "a large space of dry land comprehending many countries all joined together, without any separation by water. Thus Europe, Asia, and Africa is one great continent, as America is another."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> However, the old idea of Europe, Asia and Africa as "parts" of the world ultimately persisted with these being regarded as separate continents.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Beyond_four_continents">Beyond four continents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Continent&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Beyond four continents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thevenot_-_Hollandia_Nova_detecta_1644.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2310" data-file-width="2916" decoding="async" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Thevenot_-_Hollandia_Nova_detecta_1644.png/250px-Thevenot_-_Hollandia_Nova_detecta_1644.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Thevenot_-_Hollandia_Nova_detecta_1644.png/375px-Thevenot_-_Hollandia_Nova_detecta_1644.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Thevenot_-_Hollandia_Nova_detecta_1644.png/500px-Thevenot_-_Hollandia_Nova_detecta_1644.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thevenot_-_Hollandia_Nova_detecta_1644.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Hollandia Nova</i>, 1659 map prepared by <a href="/wiki/Joan_Blaeu" title="Joan Blaeu">Joan Blaeu</a> based on voyages by <a href="/wiki/Abel_Tasman" title="Abel Tasman">Abel Tasman</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Willem_Jansz" title="Willem Jansz">Willem Jansz</a>, this image shows a French edition of 1663</div></div></div><p>From the late 18th century, some geographers started to regard North America and South America as two parts of the world, making five parts in total. Overall though, the fourfold division prevailed well into the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lewis30_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-lewis30-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>Europeans discovered <a href="/wiki/Australia_(continent)" title="Australia (continent)">Australia</a> in 1606, but for some time it was taken as part of Asia. By the late 18th century, some geographers considered it a continent in its own right, making it the sixth (or fifth for those still taking America as a single continent).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lewis30_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-lewis30-85">[85]</a></sup> In 1813, <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Butler_(schoolmaster)" title="Samuel Butler (schoolmaster)">Samuel Butler</a> wrote of Australia as "<a href="/wiki/New_Holland_(Australia)" title="New Holland (Australia)">New Holland</a>, an immense island, which some geographers dignify with the appellation of another continent" and the <i><a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">Oxford English Dictionary</a></i> was just as equivocal some decades later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> It was in the 1950s that the concept of Oceania as a "great division" of the world was replaced by the concept of Australia as a continent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lewis32b_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-lewis32b-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a> was sighted in 1820 during the <a href="/wiki/First_Russian_Antarctic_Expedition" title="First Russian Antarctic Expedition">First Russian Antarctic Expedition</a> and described as a continent by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Wilkes" title="Charles Wilkes">Charles Wilkes</a> on the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Exploring_Expedition" title="United States Exploring Expedition">United States Exploring Expedition</a> in 1838, the last continent identified, although a great "Antarctic" (<a href="/wiki/Antipodes" title="Antipodes">antipodean</a>) landmass had been <a href="/wiki/Terra_Australis" title="Terra Australis">anticipated</a> for millennia. An 1849 atlas labelled Antarctica as a continent but few atlases did so until after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>Over time, the western concept of dividing the world into continents spread globally, replacing conceptions in other areas of the world. The idea of continents continued to become imbued with cultural and political meaning. In the 19th century during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meiji_period" title="Meiji period">Meiji period</a>, Japanese leaders began to self-identify with the concept of being Asian, and renew relations with other "Asian" countries while conceiving of the idea of Asian solidarity against western countries. This conception of an Asian identity, as well as the idea of Asian solidarity, was later taken up by others in the region, such as Republican China and Vietnam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>From the mid-19th century, atlases published in the United States more commonly treated North and South America as separate continents, while atlases published in Europe usually considered them one continent. However, it was still not uncommon for American atlases to treat them as one continent up until World War II.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lewis32_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-lewis32-90">[90]</a></sup> From the 1950s, most U.S. geographers divided the Americas into two continents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lewis32_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-lewis32-90">[90]</a></sup> With the addition of Antarctica, this made the seven-continent model. However, this division of the Americas never appealed to <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin Americans</a>, who saw their region spanning an <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">América</i></span> as a single landmass, and there the conception of six continents remains dominant, as it does in scattered other countries.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Some geographers regard Europe and Asia together as a single continent, dubbed <i><a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> In this model, the world is divided into six continents, with North America and South America considered separate continents.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geology">Geology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Continent&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Geology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Continental_crust" title="Continental crust">Continental crust</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">Plate tectonics</a></div><p>Geologists use the term <i>continent</i> in a different manner from geographers. In geology, a continent is defined by continental crust, which is a platform of <a href="/wiki/Metamorphic_rock" title="Metamorphic rock">metamorphic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Igneous_rock" title="Igneous rock">igneous rock</a>, largely of <a href="/wiki/Granite" title="Granite">granitic</a> composition. Continental crust is less dense and much thicker than oceanic crust, which causes it to "float" higher than oceanic crust on the dense underlying <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_mantle" title="Earth's mantle">mantle</a>. This explains why the continents form high platforms surrounded by deep ocean basins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glossary2005_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Glossary2005-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some geologists restrict the term 'continent' to portions of the crust built around stable regions called <i><a href="/wiki/Craton" title="Craton">cratons</a></i>. Cratons have largely been unaffected by mountain-building events (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orogenies" title="Orogenies">orogenies</a>) since the <a href="/wiki/Precambrian" title="Precambrian">Precambrian</a>. A craton typically consists of a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Continental_Shield" title="Continental Shield">continental shield</a></i> surrounded by a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Continental_platform" title="Continental platform">continental platform</a></i>. The shield is a region where ancient crystalline <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basement_rock" title="Basement rock">basement rock</a> (typically 1.5 to 3.8 billion years old) is widely exposed at the surface. The platform surrounding the shield is also composed of ancient basement rock, but with a cover of younger <a href="/wiki/Sedimentary_rock" title="Sedimentary rock">sedimentary rock</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevin201083_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevin201083-93">[93]</a></sup> The continents are accretionary crustal "rafts" that, unlike the denser basaltic crust of the ocean basins, are not subjected to destruction through the plate tectonic process of subduction. This accounts for the great age of the rocks comprising the continental cratons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevin2010194_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevin2010194-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>The margins of geologic continents are either <a href="/wiki/Passive_margin" title="Passive margin">passive</a> or <a href="/wiki/Convergent_boundary" title="Convergent boundary">active.</a> An active margin is characterised by mountain building, either through a continent-on continent collision or a subduction zone. Continents grow by <a href="/wiki/Accretion_(geology)" title="Accretion (geology)">accreting</a> lighter volcanic island chains and microcontinents along these active margins, forming <a href="/wiki/Orogenic_belt" title="Orogenic belt">orogens</a>. At a passive margin, the continental crust is stretched thin by extension to form a <a href="/wiki/Continental_shelf" title="Continental shelf">continental shelf</a>, which tapers off with a gradual slope covered in sediment, connecting it directly to the oceanic crust beyond. Most passive margins eventually transition into active margins: where the oceanic plate becomes too heavy due to cooling, it disconnects from the continental crust, and starts subducting below it, forming a new subduction zone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:392px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Plates_tect2_en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2844" data-file-width="4167" decoding="async" height="266" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Plates_tect2_en.svg/390px-Plates_tect2_en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Plates_tect2_en.svg/585px-Plates_tect2_en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Plates_tect2_en.svg/780px-Plates_tect2_en.svg.png 2x" width="390"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Plates_tect2_en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Principal tectonic plates of the continents and the floor of the oceans</div></div></div>
<p>There are many microcontinents, or <a href="/wiki/Continental_fragment" title="Continental fragment">continental fragments</a>, that are built of continental crust but do not contain a craton. Some of these are fragments of <a href="/wiki/Gondwana" title="Gondwana">Gondwana</a> or other ancient cratonic continents: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zealandia_(continent)" title="Zealandia (continent)">Zealandia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mortimer_et_al_2017_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mortimer_et_al_2017-96">[96]</a></sup> which includes <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_New_Zealand" title="Geography of New Zealand">New Zealand</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_New_Caledonia" title="Geography of New Caledonia">New Caledonia</a>; <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Madagascar" title="Geography of Madagascar">Madagascar</a>; the northern <a href="/wiki/Mascarene_Plateau" title="Mascarene Plateau">Mascarene Plateau</a>, which includes the <a href="/wiki/Seychelles" title="Seychelles">Seychelles</a>. Other islands, such as several in the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Sea" title="Caribbean Sea">Caribbean Sea</a>, are composed largely of granitic rock as well, but all continents contain both granitic and basaltic crust, and there is no clear boundary as to which islands would be considered microcontinents under such a definition. The <a href="/wiki/Kerguelen_Plateau" title="Kerguelen Plateau">Kerguelen Plateau</a>, for example, is largely volcanic, but is associated with the breakup of Gondwanaland and is considered a microcontinent,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> whereas volcanic <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Iceland" title="Geography of Iceland">Iceland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hawaiian_Islands" title="Hawaiian Islands">Hawaii</a> are not. The <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Borneo" title="Borneo">Borneo</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_(island)" title="Newfoundland (island)">Newfoundland</a> were on the margins of the <a href="/wiki/Laurasia" title="Laurasia">Laurasian</a> continent—only separated from the main continental landmass by inland seas flooding its margins.
</p><p>The movement of plates has caused the continual formation and breakup of continents, and occasionally supercontinents, in a process called the <a href="/wiki/Wilson_Cycle" title="Wilson Cycle">Wilson Cycle</a>. The supercontinent <a href="/wiki/Columbia_(supercontinent)" title="Columbia (supercontinent)">Columbia</a> or Nuna formed during a period of 2.0–1.8 billion years ago and broke up about 1.5–1.3 billion years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zhao1_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zhao1-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zhao2_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zhao2-100">[100]</a></sup> The supercontinent <a href="/wiki/Rodinia" title="Rodinia">Rodinia</a> is thought to have formed about 1 billion years ago and to have embodied most or all of Earth's continents, and broken up into eight continents around 600 million years ago. The eight continents later reassembled into another supercontinent called <a href="/wiki/Pangaea" title="Pangaea">Pangaea</a>; Pangaea broke up into Laurasia (which became North America and Eurasia) and Gondwana (which became the remaining continents).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Highest_and_lowest_points">Highest and lowest points</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Continent&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Highest and lowest points">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Seven_Summits" title="Seven Summits">Seven Summits</a></div>

<p>Some sources list the <a href="/wiki/Kuma%E2%80%93Manych_Depression" title="Kuma–Manych Depression">Kuma–Manych Depression</a> (a remnant of the <a href="/wiki/Paratethys" title="Paratethys">Paratethys</a>) as the geological border between Europe and Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Primary,_secondary_and_tertiary_sources" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable secondary sources. (October 2021)">non-tertiary source needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> This would place the Caucasus outside of Europe, thus making <a href="/wiki/Mont_Blanc" title="Mont Blanc">Mont Blanc</a> (elevation 4810 m) in the <a href="/wiki/Graian_Alps" title="Graian Alps">Graian Alps</a> the highest point in Europe – the lowest point would still be the shore of the Caspian Sea.
</p>

<li id="cite_note-Ollier-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Ollier_11-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Cliff_Ollier" title="Cliff Ollier">Ollier, Cliff D.</a> (1996). <li id="cite_note-Dempsey_2013-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Dempsey_2013_18-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1" id="CITEREFDempsey2013">Dempsey, Caitlin (15 October 2013). <a class="external text" href="https://www.geographyrealm.com/continents/" rel="nofollow">"Geography Facts about the World's Continents"</a>. <i>Geography Realm</i><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">26 August</span> 2022</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Geography+Realm&amp;rft.atitle=Geography+Facts+about+the+World%27s+Continents&amp;rft.date=2013-10-15&amp;rft.aulast=Dempsey&amp;rft.aufirst=Caitlin&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.geographyrealm.com%2Fcontinents%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AContinent"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-worldometers-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-worldometers_19-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external autonumber" href="https://www.worldometers.info/geography/continents/" rel="nofollow">[1]</a> "4 Continents
This would probably be the correct subdivision if we adopted a strict definition of continents, ideally defined as large landmasses separated by water. Furthermore, we should consider "separated" only what is naturally separated, excluding therefore the separations resulting from the artificially made Panama Canal (which separates North and South America) and Suez Canal (which separates Africa from Eurasia).

Under this model, the four continents of the world are: Afro-Eurasia (or Eurafrasia), America, and Australia (not Oceania, which combines Australia with smaller countries in the Pacific Ocean which are separated by water), and Antarctica.

An alternative four-continent model, introduced at the beginning of the 20th century, included Europe, Asia, Africa, and America."<sup class="reference plainlinks nourlexpansion" id="ref_5"><a href="#endnote_5">worldometers</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-wonderopolis-20"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-wonderopolis_20-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://www.wonderopolis.org/wonder/how-many-continents-are-there" rel="nofollow">"How many continents are there?"</a>. <i>Wonderopolis</i>. <q>If you count Europe and Asia as one continent (often known as Eurasia), then there are six continents. Some people also count North America and South America as one continent (called simply America), since the two land masses are separated only by the human-made Panama Canal. This would allow for a model with only five continents.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Wonderopolis&amp;rft.atitle=How+many+continents+are+there%3F&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.wonderopolis.org%2Fwonder%2Fhow-many-continents-are-there&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AContinent"></span><sup class="reference plainlinks nourlexpansion" id="ref_8"><a href="#endnote_8">wonderopolis</a></sup></span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-Kermack2013-26"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Kermack2013_26-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFKermack2013">Kermack, D. M. (9 March 2013). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=_-ePBAAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PA141" rel="nofollow"><i>The Evolution of Mammalian Characters</i></a>. <a href="/wiki/Springer_Science%2BBusiness_Media" title="Springer Science+Business Media">Springer Science+Business Media</a>. pp. 141–. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-4684-7817-4" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-4684-7817-4"><bdi>978-1-4684-7817-4</bdi></a> – via <a href="/wiki/Google_Books" title="Google Books">Google Books</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Evolution+of+Mammalian+Characters&amp;rft.pages=141-&amp;rft.pub=Springer+Science%2BBusiness+Media&amp;rft.date=2013-03-09&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-4684-7817-4&amp;rft.aulast=Kermack&amp;rft.aufirst=D.+M.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3D_-ePBAAAQBAJ%26pg%3DPA141&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AContinent"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-scienceline-27"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-scienceline_27-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external autonumber" href="http://scienceline.ucsb.edu/getkey.php?key=196" rel="nofollow">[2]</a> "In some textbooks, North and South America are combined into "America" and/or Europe and Asia are combined into "Eurasia", for a grant total of 6 or even 5."<sup class="reference plainlinks nourlexpansion" id="ref_7"><a href="#endnote_7">scienceline</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Wigen-28"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Wigen_28-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Martin W. Lewis and Kären E. Wigen, The Myth of Continents: A Critique of Metageography (Berkeley and Los Angeles: University of California Press, 1997)<sup class="reference plainlinks nourlexpansion" id="ref_9"><a href="#endnote_9">Wigen</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-worldometers2-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-worldometers2_29-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external autonumber" href="https://www.worldometers.info/geography/continents/" rel="nofollow">[3]</a> "5 Continents
This model adopts the criteria of both the six-continent models, resulting in the following 5 continents: Africa, Eurasia, America, Oceania (or Australia), and Antarctica.

An alternative five-continent model is the one adopted, among others, by the Olympic Charter, which excludes Antarctica as uninhabited and lists the following five: Africa, Europe, Asia, America, and Oceania (or Australia)."<sup class="reference plainlinks nourlexpansion" id="ref_10"><a href="#endnote_10">worldometers2</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-universetoday-30"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-universetoday_30-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external autonumber" href="https://www.universetoday.com/45468/how-many-continents-are-there/" rel="nofollow">[4]</a> "There are even geographical views that prefer the presence of both a Eurasian as well as one American continent. These geographers therefore contend that there should only be 5 continents."<sup class="reference plainlinks nourlexpansion" id="ref_11"><a href="#endnote_11">universetoday</a></sup></span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-61"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-61">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In Ibero-America, North America usually designates a region (<span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">subcontinente</i></span> in Spanish) of the Americas containing Canada, the United States, and Mexico, and often Greenland, Saint Pierre and Miquelon, and Bermuda; the land bridge of Central America is generally considered a subregion of North America.<a class="external text" href="https://mx.encarta.msn.com/encyclopedia_761562468/Norteam%C3%A9rica.html" rel="nofollow">Norteamérica (Mexican version)</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20090130015145/http://mx.encarta.msn.com/encyclopedia_761562468/Norteam%C3%A9rica.html" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 30 January 2009 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>/<a class="external text" href="https://es.encarta.msn.com/encyclopedia_761562468/Norteam%C3%A9rica.html" rel="nofollow">(Spaniard version)</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20090129035625/http://es.encarta.msn.com/encyclopedia_761562468/Norteam%C3%A9rica.html" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 29 January 2009 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>. <i>Encarta Online Encyclopedia</i>.. 31 October 2009.</span>

<li id="cite_note-lewis32b-87"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-lewis32b_87-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFLewis_&amp;_Wigen,_The_Myth_of_Continents1997">Lewis &amp; Wigen, The Myth of Continents (1997)</a>, p. 32: "...the 1950s... was also the period when... Oceania as a "great division" was replaced by Australia as a continent along with a series of isolated and continentally attached islands. [Footnote 78: When Southeast Asia was conceptualized as a world region during World War II..., Indonesia and the Philippines were perforce added to Asia, which reduced the extent of Oceania, leading to a reconceptualization of Australia as a continent in its own right. This maneuver is apparent in postwar atlases]"</span>
